@jwalkenrdc Those changes would also come with the associated benefits to reducing pollution -- fewer asthma attacks, fewer missed days of school, increased productivity, etc.
@jwalkenrdc Opponents, though, note that these are not phantom benefits -- if a regulation of mercury means the reduction of coal-fired power (or installation of pollution-control technology), it will necessarily mean a reduction in associated pollutants.
@jwalkenrdc They say this would inflate the benefits of regulation when there are other statutes/standards for those other pollutants. Therefore, it's more proper to use those other statutes to directly regulate those other pollutants.
@jwalkenrdc The move follows on a theme from the Trump EPA - it is trying to ditch the counting of "co-benefits."		Basically, admin &amp; industry say it's improper to count reductions in pollution outside the intended regulatory target as a benefit of regulating.

@HirokoTabuchi could make circular arguments all day. for many, it's an ideological opposition to government-funded transit projects (i.e. higher taxes). it begins and ends there. but if non-monetized externalities (pollution, congestion, etc.) aren't considered, no one's having the same convo.
@HirokoTabuchi ... the rapid rise of ride-sharing came with false claims of reducing traffic congestion. we know that hasn't been true. so to say ride-sharing is the future ignores the fact we'll probably have to innovate our way out of congestion. one way to do that? better public transit.
@HirokoTabuchi rail opponents (as noted in the story) have zeroed in on Uber, Lyft and self-driving cars as the future. it's true even in d.c. that Metro ridership is down - a combo btw bad delays from deferred maintenance and emergence of ride sharing. BUT...
@HirokoTabuchi there's reasons to distinguish unconnected light rail/street cars (i.e. DC's H St Streetcar) from actual metro systems (in DC, Chicago, NYC, etc). would be interested to know if public transit opponents make those distinctions.
@HirokoTabuchi that said, while many public transit projects are well-intentioned, having them just to have them doesn't make sense. Detroit's new Q Line collected $417k in fees in its first year and its 1.35m rides were 500,000 short of its mark. https://t.co/leqYbKSluo

Again - lots of voodoo economics with any cost-benefit analysis. I always take them with a grain of salt. But federal regulatory impact analyses have to stand up in court, too. So these are the most high stakes of cost-benefit analyses. Details on whatever emerges will matter.
Co-benefits emerged often in discussion of the Obama Clean Power Plan proposal. Opponents argued existing regulations already accounted for reductions in mercury &amp; other air pollutants, said EPA shouldn't count any reductions in those pollutants as a benefit for rule on GHGs.
A new rule, as sought by the administration's allies, would seek to restrict the counting of co-benefits. How that would be done is important. If it's too restrictive, it could undervalue threats to public health. But the devil will be in the details.
Here's why this action today matters: Conservatives &amp; industry want to eliminate counting of co-benefits (i.e., if a regulation aims to reduce GHGs, the concurrent reduction of hazardous air pollutants shouldn't count as a benefit). They claim co-benefits overstate reg benefits.
Federal agencies are supposed to be a tad more objective than interest groups. So far, the current EPA has caught a good amount of flak for shoddy analyses and assumptions about public health impacts from regs. Likewise, opponents of last EPA said it inflated benefits from regs.
To be sure, cost-benefit analyses can be configured to arrive at whatever conclusion you want. When was the last time you saw an enviro group- or industry group-funded study come out with an economic that doesn't support their policy objective?
EPA wants to change how it conducts cost-benefit analyses. 		https://t.co/yKD2QpBB2J		This could have major implications.
